2023-03-22 11:35:51 [ASSISTANT] Unfortunately, Pandas3D is not a suitable Python package for creating a driving game. If you meant "Panda3D," here's a simple driving game using Panda3D, a 3D rendering and game development library for Python.

You will need to install the `panda3d` package before running the game. To install, run the following command:

```bash
pip install panda3d
```

Below is a simple driving game using `Panda3D`.

```python
import sys
import math
from panda3d.core import Vec3
from direct.showbase.ShowBase import ShowBase
from direct.task import Task
from direct.actor.Actor import Actor
from direct.interval.IntervalGlobal import Sequence
from pandac.PandaModules import CollisionTraverser, CollisionNode, CollisionSphere, CollisionHandlerEvent


class SimpleDrivingGame(ShowBase):
    def __init__(self):
        ShowBase.__init__(self)
        self.car_speed = 20.0

        self.car = loader.loadModel("models/cartoon_car")  # Replace with the model that you want to use
        self.car.reparentTo(render)
        self.car.setScale(0.07)
        self.car.setPos(0, 0, 0)

        self.camera.setPos(0, -25, 10)
        self.camera.setHpr(0, -15, 0)

        self.taskMgr.add(self.movement_task, "MovementTask")

        self.accept("escape", sys.exit)
        self.accept("arrow_up", self.set_speed, [self.car_speed])
        self.accept("arrow_up-up", self.set_speed, [0])
        self.accept("arrow_down", self.set_speed, [-self.car_speed])
        self.accept("arrow_down-up", self.set_speed, [0])
        self.accept("arrow_left", self.turn_car, [20])
        self.accept("arrow_left-up", self.turn_car, [0])
        self.accept("arrow_right", self.turn_car, [-20])
        self.accept("arrow_right-up", self.turn_car, [0])

    def set_speed(self, speed):
        self.car_speed = speed

    def turn_car(self, delta_angle):
        self.car.setH(self.car.getH() + delta_angle)

    def movement_task(self, task):
        dt = globalClock.getDt()
        dist = self.car_speed * dt

        car_delta = Vec3(math.sin(math.radians(self.car.getH())) * dist, math.cos(math.radians(self.car.getH())) * dist, 0)

        self.camera.setPos(self.camera.getPos() + car_delta)
        self.car.setPos(self.car.getPos() + car_delta)

        return Task.cont


if __name__ == "__main__":
    game = SimpleDrivingGame()
    game.run()
```

Please note that you will need to replace `"models/cartoon_car"` with the path to the 3D model you want to use for the car in the game. You can find free 3D models online on websites like [Turbosquid](https://www.turbosquid.com/) or [Sketchfab](https://sketchfab.com/). Additionally, the game's physics and mechanics are very basic and can be further improved to build a more complete driving game.

2023-03-22 11:36:50 [ASSISTANT] I apologize for the error in the code. The issue is caused by the fact that the model file `models/cartoon_car` cannot be found. Since there isn't an actual 3D model file provided in the code, you just need to replace this path with a suitable 3D model file for the car.

If you don't have your own 3D model file, you can download a free model from websites like [Turbosquid](https://www.turbosquid.com/) or [Sketchfab](https://sketchfab.com/). Once you have a car model file, you need to:

1. Convert it to a format that Panda3D supports (e.g., `.egg` or `.bam`).
2. Place the 3D model file in the same directory as your Python script or provide an absolute path to the model.
3. Replace `"models/cartoon_car"` in the code with the path to your 3D model file.

As an example, after downloading a car model and converting it to the `.bam` format, you could place it in the same directory as your Python script and change the path in the code to `"my_car_model.bam"` like this:

```python
self.car = loader.loadModel("my_car_model.bam")  # Replace with the path to your 3D model file
```

### Instructions for converting 3D models

You can use the [Blender software](https://www.blender.org/) to convert 3D models to the `.egg` or `.bam` format.

1. Install Blender and the [Panda3D plugin for Blender](https://github.com/rdb/blender-panda).
2. Open the 3D model file in Blender.
3. Save the file in the `.egg` or `.bam` format using "File" > "Export" > "Panda3D (.egg)" or "Panda3D (.bam)".

Now you can use the converted model in your Panda3D project.

Remember to keep in mind that the game's physics and mechanics are very basic and can be further improved to build a more complete driving game.

2023-03-22 11:40:55 [ASSISTANT] To plot Tesla's stock price for the past 30 days, you'll need to first get the stock data using a market data provider, such as Yahoo Finance. You can use the `pandas`, `pandas-datareader`, `datetime`, and `matplotlib` libraries to accomplish this.

Ensure you have the required dependencies installed. You can use the following command to install them:

```bash
pip install pandas pandas-datareader matplotlib
```

Here's a Python script that uses these libraries to plot Tesla's 30-day stock price history:

```python
import pandas as pd
import pandas_datareader as pdr
import datetime as dt
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

# Set the end date to today and start date to 30 days ago
end_date = dt.date.today()
start_date = end_date - dt.timedelta(days=30)

# Fetch Tesla's stock data
ticker = "TSLA"
stock_data = pdr.get_data_yahoo(ticker, start_date, end_date)

# Plot the stock data
plt.figure(figsize=(12,6))
plt.plot(stock_data.index, stock_data["Close"], label=ticker)
plt.xlabel("Date")
plt.ylabel("Close Price")
plt.title(f"{ticker} Stock Price History (Last 30 days)")
plt.legend()
plt.show()
```

Run this script and it will plot the 30-day stock price history for Tesla.

2023-03-22 11:44:07 [ASSISTANT] To predict the price of TESLA for the next 30 days, we will be using the historical data of TESLA stock prices and create a simple time series model using the FBProphet library. First, we need to install the required dependencies:

```bash
pip install pandas yfinance fbprophet
```

Now, let's write the Python code to fetch historical data, preprocess it, and perform the prediction:

```python
import pandas as pd
import yfinance as yf
from fbprophet import Prophet

# Fetching TESLA historical data
symbol = "TSLA"
start_date = "2022-01-01"
end_date = "2022-12-31"
data = yf.download(symbol, start=start_date, end=end_date)
data.reset_index(inplace=True)

# Preparing data for Prophet model
data = data[["Date", "Close"]]
data.columns = ["ds", "y"]

# Create and fit the Prophet model
model = Prophet(daily_seasonality=True)
model.fit(data)

# Predicting TESLA price for the next 30 days
future = model.make_future_dataframe(periods=30)
forecast = model.predict(future)

# Filter the predictions for the next 30 days
next_30_days = forecast.tail(30)[["ds", "yhat", "yhat_lower", "yhat_upper"]]
print(next_30_days)
```

Just copy and run the Python code above, and you'll get the predicted TESLA price for the next 30 days. Note that this is a simple model, and actual stock prices might differ significantly from the predictions. Always consult with financial professionals before making any investment decisions.

2023-03-22 11:48:33 [ASSISTANT] To create a FastAPI application that exposes all tables in a given database, you'll first need to install the following dependencies in your project environment:

```bash
pip install fastapi uvicorn sqlalchemy databases
```

Now, let's create a FastAPI application:

```python
from sqlalchemy import create_engine, MetaData, Table
from sqlalchemy.engine.url import URL
from databases import Database

from fastapi import FastAPI

# Update the following database settings to fit your database configuration
DATABASE_SETTINGS = {
    "drivername": "postgresql",
    "host": "localhost",
    "port": "5432",
    "username": "your_username",
    "password": "your_password",
    "database": "your_database",
}

app = FastAPI()
db = Database(URL(**DATABASE_SETTINGS))
engine = create_engine(URL(**DATABASE_SETTINGS))


async def fetch_tables():
    meta = MetaData()
    meta.reflect(bind=engine)

    tables = []
    for table_name, table_obj in meta.tables.items():
        table = {"name": table_name, "columns": [col.name for col in table_obj.columns]}
        tables.append(table)

    return tables


@app.on_event("startup")
async def startup():
    await db.connect()


@app.on_event("shutdown")
async def shutdown():
    await db.disconnect()


@app.get("/tables")
async def get_tables():
    return await fetch_tables()


if __name__ == "__main__":
    import uvicorn

    uvicorn.run(app, host="127.0.0.1", port=8000)
```

This FastAPI application connects to a PostgreSQL database using the provided database settings. Replace the `DATABASE_SETTINGS` values with your own. When you call the endpoint `/tables`, it will fetch and display all tables with their column names in the connected database.

To run the application:

```bash
uvicorn main:app --reload
```

Now you can visit `http://127.0.0.1:8000/tables` to see the list of tables and their columns.

```python
import requests

def get_eth_price_gbp():
    url = "https://api.coingecko.com/api/v3/simple/price?ids=ethereum&vs_currencies=gbp"
    response = requests.get(url)
    data = response.json()

    return eth_price_gbp
```

An easy way to get the price of Ethereum (ETH) in GBP (British Pounds) from a public web API is to use CoinGecko's API. Here is how you can do it using Python and the `requests` library.

First, install the 'requests' library if you don't have it already:

```bash
pip install requests
```

Next, create a script with the following code:

```python
import requests

def get_eth_gbp_price():
    url = 'https://api.coingecko.com/api/v3/simple/price?ids=ethereum&vs_currencies=gbp'
    response = requests.get(url)
    
    if response.status_code == 200:
        json_data = response.json()
        eth_gbp = json_data['ethereum']['gbp']
        return eth_gbp
    else:
        return None

eth_gbp_price = get_eth_gbp_price()
if eth_gbp_price is not None:
    print(f"The current price of 1 ETH in GBP is: £{eth_gbp_price}")
else:
    print("Unable to fetch the ETH price in GBP.")
```

When you run this script, it will fetch the price of Ethereum in GBP from the CoinGecko API and print it on the screen.

Remember that the fetched data is subject to rate limits imposed by the API provider. You may want to verify those limits in CoinGecko's API documentation.

2023-03-22 12:10:34 [ASSISTANT] I cannot execute code or access external APIs directly, but I can help you create a Python script to fetch the last 30 days ETH prices in GBP and plot it using the Pandas and Matplotlib libraries.

```bash
pip install requests pandas matplotlib
```

Next, you can use the following Python script, which fetches historical data using CoinGecko's API and plots the price:

```python
import requests
import pandas as pd
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
from datetime import datetime, timedelta

def get_eth_historical_price(days=30):
    end_date = datetime.now()
    start_date = end_date - timedelta(days=days)
    start_date_str = start_date.strftime('%d-%m-%Y')
    end_date_str = end_date.strftime('%d-%m-%Y')
    
    url = f'https://api.coingecko.com/api/v3/coins/ethereum/market_chart/range?vs_currency=gbp&from={start_date.timestamp()}&to={end_date.timestamp()}'
    response = requests.get(url)
    
    if response.status_code == 200:
        json_data = response.json()
        return json_data
    else:
        return None

def plot_eth_price(data):
    df = pd.DataFrame(data['prices'], columns=['timestamp', 'price'])
    df['date'] = pd.to_datetime(df['timestamp'], unit='ms')
    df.set_index('date', inplace=True)

    plt.plot(df.index, df['price'])
    plt.xlabel('Date')
    plt.ylabel('Price (GBP)')
    plt.title('Ethereum Price in GBP for the Last 30 Days')
    plt.xticks(rotation=45)
    plt.grid()
    plt.show()

historical_data = get_eth_historical_price(days=30)
if historical_data is not None:
    plot_eth_price(historical_data)
else:
    print("Unable to fetch historical data.")
```

This script fetches the historical price data for Ethereum in GBP for the past 30 days and displays a plotted graph using the Pandas and Matplotlib libraries. You can modify the `days` parameter to fetch data for a different duration. Keep in mind that the fetched data is subject to rate limits imposed by the API provider, CoinGecko. You may want to check those limits in their API documentation.


Keep in mind that this API request might not give you exactly 30 days worth of data in a single request. You might have to paginate through the response and make multiple requests to get the entire dataset, then calculate the floor price.

Please note that BAYC is a collection of NFTs, so the floor price will vary for different NFTs in the collection. To get a more accurate floor price for the entire collection, you will need to fetch data for multiple BAYC NFTs and calculate an average floor price across these NFTs.

```python
# Prepare the API request URL
```

2023-03-22 12:17:27 [ASSISTANT] Creating a full 3D game requires much more code than can be provided in this answer. However, I can suggest using the `Panda3D` game engine, which is designed for Python programming, and provide you with a simple example to get started.

To begin, you need to install Panda3D game engine:

```bash
pip install panda3d
```

Then, you can create a basic game script (e.g., `panda3d_game.py`) with the following example, which creates a scene with a box:

```python
from direct.showbase.ShowBase import ShowBase
from panda3d.core import Box, Geom, GeomNode, GeomTriangles, GeomVertexData, GeomVertexFormat, GeomVertexWriter

class MyApp(ShowBase):
    def __init__(self):
        ShowBase.__init__(self)

        format = GeomVertexFormat.get_v3()
        data = GeomVertexData("box", format, Geom.UH_static)
        data.set_num_rows(8)

        vertex = GeomVertexWriter(data, "vertex")
        vertex.add_data3(-1, -1, -1)
        vertex.add_data3(1, -1, -1)
        vertex.add_data3(1, 1, -1)
        vertex.add_data3(-1, 1, -1)
        vertex.add_data3(-1, -1, 1)
        vertex.add_data3(1, -1, 1)
        vertex.add_data3(1, 1, 1)
        vertex.add_data3(-1, 1, 1)

        geom = Geom(data)

        for i, j in ((0, 1), (1, 2), (2, 3), (3, 0)):
            tri = GeomTriangles(Geom.UH_static)
            tri.add_vertices(i, i + 4, j + 4)
            tri.add_vertices(i, j + 4, j)
            geom.add_primitive(tri)

        node = GeomNode("box")
        node.add_geom(geom)
        box_np = self.render.attach_new_node(node)

        box_np.set_pos(-4, 0, 3)
        box_np.set_hpr(45, 15, 0)

        self.cam.set_pos(10, -20, 10)
        self.cam.look_at(0, 0, 0)
        
        self.disable_mouse()

app = MyApp()
app.run()
```

This script creates a basic window with a 3D box in the scene. To execute the script, simply run:

```
python panda3d_game.py
```

This is only a starting point. To create a full game, you will likely want to set up input handling, animations, collision detection, and more. Refer to the Panda3D documentation (https://docs.panda3d.org/1.10/python/index) to learn about various aspects of creating a 3D game using Panda3D in Python.

```python
from direct.showbase.ShowBase import ShowBase
from panda3d.core import Box, Geom, GeomNode, GeomTriangles, GeomVertexData, GeomVertexFormat, GeomVertexWriter

class MyApp(ShowBase):
    def __init__(self):
        ShowBase.__init__(self)

        format = GeomVertexFormat.get_v3()
        data = GeomVertexData("box", format, Geom.UH_static)
        data.set_num_rows(8)

        vertex = GeomVertexWriter(data, "vertex")
        vertex.add_data3(-1, -1, -1)
        vertex.add_data3(1, -1, -1)
        vertex.add_data3(1, 1, -1)
        vertex.add_data3(-1, 1, -1)
        vertex.add_data3(-1, -1, 1)
        vertex.add_data3(1, -1, 1)
        vertex.add_data3(1, 1, 1)
        vertex.add_data3(-1, 1, 1)

        geom = Geom(data)

        for i, j in ((0, 1), (1, 2), (2, 3), (3, 0)):
            tri = GeomTriangles(Geom.UH_static)
            tri.add_vertices(i, i + 4, j + 4)
            tri.add_vertices(i, j + 4, j)
            geom.add_primitive(tri)

        node = GeomNode("box")
        node.add_geom(geom)
        box_np = self.render.attach_new_node(node)

        box_np.set_pos(-4, 0, 3)
        box_np.set_hpr(45, 15, 0)

        self.cam.set_pos(10, -20, 10)
        self.cam.look_at(0, 0, 0)
        
        self.disable_mouse()

app = MyApp()
```

2023-03-22 12:21:51 [ASSISTANT] I'm an AI language model and cannot directly fetch live data. However, I can help you with example code that you can use to fetch live BTC price and to plot it. Here's some example code for fetching price data and plotting it, you can use Python and the libraries requests, pandas, and matplotlib for this purpose.

First, you need to install the required libraries if you haven't done that already:
```sh
pip install requests pandas matplotlib
```

Then you can use the following code to fetch the data and plot it:

```python
import requests
import pandas as pd
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

# Function to get the BTC price in GBP for the last 30 days
def get_btc_price_30_days(api_key):
    url = f'https://min-api.cryptocompare.com/data/v2/histoday?fsym=BTC&tsym=GBP&limit=30&api_key={api_key}'
    response = requests.get(url)
    
    if response.status_code == 200:
        data = response.json()
        return data['Data']['Data']
    else:
        print(f'Error: {response.status_code}')
        return None

# Replace 'YOUR_API_KEY' with your actual CryptoCompare API key
api_key = 'YOUR_API_KEY'
btc_price_data = get_btc_price_30_days(api_key)

if btc_price_data:
    # Convert the data to a pandas DataFrame and plot the chart
    btc_df = pd.DataFrame(btc_price_data)
    btc_df['time'] = pd.to_datetime(btc_df['time'], unit='s')
    btc_df.set_index('time', inplace=True)
    
    plt.figure(figsize=(12, 6))
    plt.plot(btc_df['close'])
    plt.xlabel('Date (Last 30 Days)')
    plt.ylabel('BTC Price in GBP')
    plt.title('BTC Price in GBP for the Last 30 Days')
    plt.grid(True)
    plt.show()
else:
    print('Failed to fetch pricing data.')
```

Replace `'YOUR_API_KEY'` with your actual CryptoCompare API key, which you can obtain after signing up at: https://min-api.cryptocompare.com/

This code fetches the BTC price in GBP for the last 30 days and plots it using the Pandas and Matplotlib libraries.

2023-03-22 12:29:12 [ASSISTANT] To get the price of Bitcoin in £ (GBP) from a public web API using Python, you can use the requests module to send an HTTP GET request to the endpoint of the API and retrieve the response data in JSON format. Here's an example code that uses the Coinbase Pro API:

```python
import requests

# Coinbase Pro API endpoint for BTC-GBP pair
url = "https://api.pro.coinbase.com/products/BTC-GBP/ticker"

# Send HTTP GET request to the endpoint and retrieve JSON response
response = requests.get(url).json()

# Extract the BTC price in GBP from the response
btc_gbp_price = float(response['price'])

# Print the price
print(f"The current Bitcoin price in GBP is: {btc_gbp_price} GBP")
```

This code fetches the current BTC price in GBP from the Coinbase Pro API endpoint, extracts the price value from the JSON response, converts it to a float data type, and finally prints out the price formatted as a string with the GBP symbol.

2023-03-22 14:20:39 [ASSISTANT] Sure, to get ETH's price for the last 30 days and store it in a database, you will need to use an API that provides historical data for cryptocurrencies. Here's a code that uses the CoinGecko API to get the price of ETH for the last 30 days and store it in a MySQL database:

First, you need to install the `mysql-connector-python` library for connecting to the MySQL database using the following command in your terminal:

```
pip install mysql-connector-python
```

Then, you can use the following Python code:

```
import requests
import mysql.connector
from datetime import datetime, timedelta

# MySQL connection details
hostname = "localhost"
username = "root"
password = "password"
database = "crypto"

# Connect to the MySQL database
mydb = mysql.connector.connect(
  host=hostname,
  user=username,
  password=password,
  database=database
)
mycursor = mydb.cursor()

# URL of the API
url = "https://api.coingecko.com/api/v3/coins/ethereum/market_chart?vs_currency=gbp&days=30"

# Get the response from the API
response = requests.get(url)

# Convert the response to JSON format
data = response.json()

# Get the price data for ETH for the last 30 days
prices = data['prices']

# Store the data in the database
for price in prices:
    date = datetime.fromtimestamp(price[0] / 1000.0) # Convert the timestamp to a datetime object
    eth_price_gbp = price[1]
    sql = "INSERT INTO eth_prices (date, price) VALUES (%s, %s)"
    val = (date, eth_price_gbp)
    mycursor.execute(sql, val)

# Commit the changes to the database
mydb.commit()

# Close the MySQL connection
mycursor.close()
mydb.close()
```

This code retrieves the price of ETH in GBP for the last 30 days using the CoinGecko API and stores the data in a MySQL database. The `eth_prices` table should already exist in the `crypto` database before running this script.

The `prices` data returned by the API is a list of tuples in the format `(timestamp, price)`, where `timestamp` is the Unix timestamp in milliseconds and `price` is the price of ETH in GBP at that timestamp. The code converts the timestamp to a `datetime` object for easier handling and stores the timestamp and price in the `eth_prices` table in the MySQL database.

Note that you should replace the `hostname`, `username`, and `password` variables with the actual credentials for your MySQL database.

To get the price of ETH in £ using Python, we need to do the following steps: 

1. Install the following dependencies:
    - requests
    - json

2. Use the CoinMarketCap API to fetch the current price of ETH in GBP.
3. Parse the response for the current price of ETH in GBP.
4. Display the price in GBP.


Here is the Python code for getting the price of ETH in £: 
```python
# Import required libraries
import requests
import json

# Define CoinMarketCap API URL
url = 'https://pro-api.coinmarketcap.com/v1/cryptocurrency/quotes/latest'

# Define parameters for the API request
parameters = {
    'symbol': 'ETH',
    'convert': 'GBP'
}

# Define headers for the API request
headers = {
    'Accepts': 'application/json',
    'X-CMC_PRO_API_KEY': 'your_api_key'
}

# Send the API request
response = requests.get(url, headers=headers, params=parameters)
data = json.loads(response.text)

# Parse the response for the current ETH price in GBP
eth_price_gbp = data['data']['ETH']['quote']['GBP']['price']

# Display the current ETH price in GBP
print(f"The current price of ETH in GBP is £{eth_price_gbp:.2f}")
```

Note: You will need to replace `'your_api_key'` in the `headers` definition with your own API key. You can obtain an API key by signing up for a free CoinMarketCap account and following their API documentation.

To run this code, save the code above into a file called `eth_price.py` on your local machine. Then, in a terminal or command prompt, navigate to the directory where you saved the file and run the following command:

```
python eth_price.py
```

If everything runs correctly, you should see the current price of ETH in £ displayed in your terminal/command prompt output.

```python
def get_eth_price_gbp():
```

2023-03-22 14:40:35 [ASSISTANT] To get the price of Ethereum (ETH) in GBP for the last 90 days and plot it, you can use the `requests` and `matplotlib` libraries in Python. Here's how you can do it with the CoinGecko API:

Then, here's the Python code:

```python
import requests
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
from datetime import datetime, timedelta

def get_eth_prices_for_past_90_days():
    end_date = datetime.now()
    start_date = end_date - timedelta(days=90)
    start_timestamp = int(start_date.timestamp())
    end_timestamp = int(end_date.timestamp())
    
    url = f"https://api.coingecko.com/api/v3/coins/ethereum/market_chart/range?vs_currency=gbp&from={start_timestamp}&to={end_timestamp}"

    response = requests.get(url)
    data = response.json()
    
    eth_prices_gbp = [price_point[1] for price_point in data["prices"]]
    timestamp_dates = [price_point[0] for price_point in data["prices"]]
    
    return timestamp_dates, eth_prices_gbp

def plot_eth_prices(timestamp_dates, eth_prices_gbp):
    dates = [datetime.fromtimestamp(ts // 1000) for ts in timestamp_dates]
    
    plt.plot(dates, eth_prices_gbp)
    plt.xlabel("Date")
    plt.ylabel("Ethereum (ETH) Price in GBP")
    plt.title("Ethereum (ETH) Price in GBP for the Past 90 Days")
    plt.xticks(rotation=45)
    plt.grid()
    plt.tight_layout()
    plt.show()

timestamp_dates, eth_prices_gbp = get_eth_prices_for_past_90_days()
plot_eth_prices(timestamp_dates, eth_prices_gbp)
```

Instructions to run the code:

1. Save the code in a file named `eth_price_90_days.py`.
2. Open a terminal or command prompt.
3. Make sure you have installed the `requests` and `matplotlib` libraries using `pip install requests matplotlib`.
4. Navigate to the folder where you saved the `eth_price_90_days.py` file.
5. Run the code using `python eth_price_90_days.py` or `python3 eth_price_90_days.py` (depending on your system).
6. The code will display a plot of the Ethereum (ETH) price in GBP for the last 90 days.

```python
import requests
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
from datetime import datetime, timedelta

def get_crypto_prices_for_past_2_years(crypto_id):
    end_date = datetime.now()
    start_date = end_date - timedelta(days=365*2)
    start_timestamp = int(start_date.timestamp())
    end_timestamp = int(end_date.timestamp())
    
    url = f"https://api.coingecko.com/api/v3/coins/{crypto_id}/market_chart/range?vs_currency=gbp&from={start_timestamp}&to={end_timestamp}"

    response = requests.get(url)
    data = response.json()
    
    crypto_prices_gbp = [price_point[1] for price_point in data["prices"]]
    timestamp_dates = [price_point[0] for price_point in data["prices"]]
    
    return timestamp_dates, crypto_prices_gbp

def plot_crypto_prices(timestamp_dates, eth_prices_gbp, btc_prices_gbp):
    dates = [datetime.fromtimestamp(ts // 1000) for ts in timestamp_dates]
    
    plt.plot(dates, eth_prices_gbp, label="Ethereum (ETH)")
    plt.plot(dates, btc_prices_gbp, label="Bitcoin (BTC)")
    plt.xlabel("Date")
    plt.ylabel("Price in GBP")
    plt.title("Ethereum (ETH) vs. Bitcoin (BTC) Prices in GBP for the Past 2 Years")
    plt.xticks(rotation=45)
    plt.legend()
    plt.grid()
    plt.tight_layout()
    plt.show()

timestamp_dates, eth_prices_gbp = get_crypto_prices_for_past_2_years("ethereum")
_, btc_prices_gbp = get_crypto_prices_for_past_2_years("bitcoin")
plot_crypto_prices(timestamp_dates, eth_prices_gbp, btc_prices_gbp)
```

Instructions to run the code:

1. Save the code in a file named `eth_btc_price_2_years.py`.

4. Navigate to the folder where you saved the `eth_btc_price_2_years.py` file.
5. Run the code using `python eth_btc_price_2_years.py` or `python3 eth_btc_price_2_years.py` (depending on your system).
6. The code will display a plot comparing the prices of Ethereum (ETH) and Bitcoin (BTC) in GBP for the last 2 years.

2023-03-22 14:45:00 [ASSISTANT] To predict the prices of Ethereum (ETH) and Bitcoin (BTC) for the next 30 days, you can try using the `fbprophet` library, a forecasting tool developed by Facebook. Note that this is a simple prediction and may not be accurate.

First, install the required dependencies:

```bash
pip install requests pandas matplotlib fbprophet
```

Then, here's the Python code:

```python
import requests
import pandas as pd
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
from fbprophet import Prophet
from datetime import datetime, timedelta

def get_crypto_prices_for_past_2_years(crypto_id):
    end_date = datetime.now()
    start_date = end_date - timedelta(days=365*2)
    start_timestamp = int(start_date.timestamp())
    end_timestamp = int(end_date.timestamp())
    
    url = f"https://api.coingecko.com/api/v3/coins/{crypto_id}/market_chart/range?vs_currency=gbp&from={start_timestamp}&to={end_timestamp}"

    response = requests.get(url)
    data = response.json()
    
    crypto_prices_gbp = [price_point[1] for price_point in data["prices"]]
    timestamp_dates = [price_point[0] for price_point in data["prices"]]
    
    return timestamp_dates, crypto_prices_gbp

def make_prophet_prediction(timestamp_dates, crypto_prices_gbp, days_to_predict):
    dates = [datetime.fromtimestamp(ts // 1000) for ts in timestamp_dates]

    data = {
        "ds": dates,
        "y": crypto_prices_gbp
    }
    df = pd.DataFrame(data)

    model = Prophet()
    model.fit(df)

    future = model.make_future_dataframe(periods=days_to_predict)
    forecast = model.predict(future)

    return forecast

def plot_prophet_forecast(forecast, crypto_name):
    fig = model.plot(forecast, xlabel="Date", ylabel=f"{crypto_name} Price in GBP")
    plt.title(f"{crypto_name} Price Prediction for the Next 30 Days")
    plt.grid()
    plt.tight_layout()
    plt.show()

days_to_predict = 30

timestamp_dates, eth_prices_gbp = get_crypto_prices_for_past_2_years("ethereum")
eth_forecast = make_prophet_prediction(timestamp_dates, eth_prices_gbp, days_to_predict)
plot_prophet_forecast(eth_forecast, "Ethereum (ETH)")

timestamp_dates, btc_prices_gbp = get_crypto_prices_for_past_2_years("bitcoin")
btc_forecast = make_prophet_prediction(timestamp_dates, btc_prices_gbp, days_to_predict)
plot_prophet_forecast(btc_forecast, "Bitcoin (BTC)")
```

Instructions to run the code:

1. Save the code in a file named `eth_btc_price_prediction.py`.
2. Open a terminal or command prompt.
3. Make sure you have installed the `requests`, `pandas`, `matplotlib`, and `fbprophet` libraries using `pip install requests pandas matplotlib fbprophet`.
4. Navigate to the folder where you saved the `eth_btc_price_prediction.py` file.
5. Run the code using `python eth_btc_price_prediction.py` or `python3 eth_btc_price_prediction.py` (depending on your system).
6. The code will display plots predicting the prices of Ethereum (ETH) and Bitcoin (BTC) in GBP for the next 30 days.

Keep in mind that this is a simple time-series forecasting method and may not accurately predict future prices. Actual prices can be influenced by many factors that this approach does not account for, such as market news, regulations, and global events.

Now, create the FastAPI application:

```python
from fastapi import FastAPI
from sqlalchemy import create_engine, MetaData, Table
from sqlalchemy.sql import select

app = FastAPI()
engine = create_engine("sqlite:///test_db.db")

@app.get("/tables/{table_name}")
async def get_table(table_name: str):
    meta = MetaData()
    meta.reflect(bind=engine)
    table = meta.tables.get(table_name)

    if table:
        with engine.connect() as conn:
            response = conn.execute(select([table])).fetchall()
        
        data = [dict(zip(row.keys(), row)) for row in response]
        return {"data": data}
    
    return {"error": f"Table '{table_name}' not found"}

@app.get("/tables/list")
async def get_tables_list():
    meta = MetaData()
    meta.reflect(bind=engine)
    
    tables = [table for table in meta.tables]
    return {"tables": tables}
```

Save the above code in a file named `main.py`.

To run the FastAPI application, execute the following command in your terminal:

```bash
uvicorn main:app --reload
```

This will start the FastAPI application on `http://127.0.0.1:8000` exposing the SQLite database tables.

To view the list of available tables, go to `http://127.0.0.1:8000/tables/list`.

To view the contents of a specific table (e.g., people), go to `http://127.0.0.1:8000/tables/people`.

Replace `people` with the table name you want to view.

2023-03-22 15:04:36 [ASSISTANT] Creating a full FPS game with Panda3D will require a series of steps and extensive work. Below is an outline of the basic structure and code to kickstart your game development. You can extend it with more sophisticated functionality as needed. To begin, install the required dependency:

```bash
pip install panda3d
```

After installing Panda3D, create a new Python file, e.g. `fps_pandas3d.py`, and copy the contents below into it:

```python
import sys
from direct.showbase.ShowBase import ShowBase
from direct.actor.Actor import Actor
from panda3d.core import AmbientLight, DirectionalLight
from panda3d.core import Vec3, Vec4
from panda3d.core import CollisionTraverser, CollisionHandlerQueue, CollisionNode, CollisionSphere
from panda3d.core import WindowProperties


class FPSGame(ShowBase):
    def __init__(self):
        ShowBase.__init__(self)

        # Disable Mouse Control
        self.disable_mouse()

        # Setting up window properties
        window_properties = WindowProperties()
        window_properties.set_size(1280, 720)
        self.win.request_properties(window_properties)

        # Change the camera position
        self.camera.set_pos(0, -20, 2)

        # Load model and texture for the environment
        self.environment_model = self.loader.load_model("models/misc/ground")
        self.environment_model.reparent_to(self.render)
        self.environment_model.set_scale(40, 40, 1)
        self.environment_texture = self.loader.load_texture("models/misc/grid.png")
        self.environment_model.set_texture(self.environment_texture, 1)

        # Load a character
        self.character = Actor("models/player/zombie")
        self.character.reparent_to(self.render)
        self.character.set_pos(0, 15, 0)

        # Add Ambient Light
        ambient_light = AmbientLight("ambientLight")
        ambient_light.set_color(Vec4(0.2, 0.2, 0.2, 1))
        self.ambient_light_node_path = self.render.attach_new_node(ambient_light)
        self.render.set_light(self.ambient_light_node_path)

        # Add Directional Light
        directional_light = DirectionalLight("directionalLight")
        directional_light.set_color(Vec4(0.6, 0.6, 0.6, 1))
        self.directional_light_node_path = self.render.attach_new_node(directional_light)
        self.directional_light_node_path.set_hpr(0, -30, 0)
        self.render.set_light(self.directional_light_node_path)

        # Create a task manager to handle events continuously
        self.task_mgr.add(self.update_task, "update")

        # Create a traverser for collisions
        self.collision_traverser = CollisionTraverser()

        # Create a handler for collisions
        self.collision_handler = CollisionHandlerQueue()

        # Set up a CollisionSphere for the character
        self.character_coll_sphere = CollisionSphere(0, 1, 1, 1)
        self.character_coll_node = CollisionNode("character collision")
        self.character_coll_node.add_solid(self.character_coll_sphere)
        self.character_coll_node_path = self.character.attach_new_node(self.character_coll_node)
        self.character_coll_node.set_from_collide_mask(0x1)
        self.character_coll_node.set_into_collide_mask(0)

        # Add the character collision to the traverser
        self.collision_traverser.add_collider(self.character_coll_node_path, self.collision_handler)

    def update_task(self, task):
        # Check collisions
        self.collision_traverser.traverse(self.render)

        for entries in self.collision_handler.entries:
            # Do some game logic here, e.g., handle character interactions, add points, or change levels

            pass

        return task.cont


game = FPSGame()
game.run()
```

To run the code:

```python
import sys
from direct.showbase.ShowBase import ShowBase
from direct.actor.Actor import Actor
```

2023-03-22 15:09:03 [ASSISTANT] Let's add basic user controls for moving the camera in the game. We'll implement the following controls:

- W: Move forward
- S: Move backward
- A: Move left
- D: Move right
- Mouse: Rotate the camera

Update the code in `fps_pandas3d.py` as follows:

```python
from math import radians, sin, cos
from direct.showbase.ShowBase import ShowBase
from direct.actor.Actor import Actor
from panda3d.core import AmbientLight, DirectionalLight
from panda3d.core import Vec3, Vec4
from panda3d.core import CollisionTraverser, CollisionHandlerQueue, CollisionNode, CollisionSphere
from panda3d.core import WindowProperties, MouseButton


class FPSGame(ShowBase):
    def __init__(self):
        ShowBase.__init__(self)

        # Variables to store the speeds of camera movement and rotation
        self.movement_speed = 0.1
        self.rotation_speed = 1.0
        self.hpr = self.camera.get_hpr()

        # Disable Mouse Control
        self.disable_mouse()

        # Setting up window properties
        window_properties = WindowProperties()
        window_properties.set_size(1280, 720)
        self.win.request_properties(window_properties)

        # Change the camera position
        self.camera.set_pos(0, -5, 2)

        # Load built-in model and texture for the environment
        self.environment_model = self.loader.load_model("environment")
        self.environment_model.reparent_to(self.render)

        # Load a built-in character
        self.character = Actor("panda")
        self.character.reparent_to(self.render)
        self.character.set_pos(0, 15, 0)

        # ... (Add Ambient Light, Directional Light, Collision Traverser, etc., as before)

        # Accept keyboard and mouse events
        self.accept("w", self.set_movement_direction, [Vec3(0, 1, 0)])
        self.accept("s", self.set_movement_direction, [Vec3(0, -1, 0)])
        self.accept("a", self.set_movement_direction, [Vec3(-1, 0, 0)])
        self.accept("d", self.set_movement_direction, [Vec3(1, 0, 0)])
        self.accept("w-up", self.set_movement_direction, [Vec3(0, 0, 0)])
        self.accept("s-up", self.set_movement_direction, [Vec3(0, 0, 0)])
        self.accept("a-up", self.set_movement_direction, [Vec3(0, 0, 0)])
        self.accept("d-up", self.set_movement_direction, [Vec3(0, 0, 0)])
        self.accept("mouse1", self.rotate_camera)

        # Create a task manager to handle events continuously
        self.task_mgr.add(self.update_task, "update")

    def set_movement_direction(self, direction):
        self.movement_direction = direction

    def move_camera(self):
        self.camera.set_pos(self.camera.get_pos() + self.movement_direction * self.movement_speed)

    def rotate_camera(self):
        if self.mouseWatcherNode.has_mouse():
            dx = self.mouseWatcherNode.get_mouse_x() * self.rotation_speed
            dy = self.mouseWatcherNode.get_mouse_y() * self.rotation_speed

            self.hpr += Vec3(dy, 0, -dx)
            self.camera.set_hpr(self.hpr)

    def update_task(self, task):
        # Move and rotate the camera
        self.move_camera()
        self.rotate_camera()

        # ... (Check collisions and game logic as before)

        return task.cont


game = FPSGame()
game.run()
```

Now, with these new controls, you'll be able to move the camera using the W, S, A, D keys and rotate it by clicking and dragging the mouse while holding down the left mouse button.

Please note that this basic implementation can be extended with more advanced controls and features, such as incorporating more sophisticated character interactions or user interfaces for an improved FPS gaming experience.

2023-03-22 15:45:06 [ASSISTANT] First, let's make sure we've got the necessary dependencies installed. You'll need requests and sqlalchemy libraries. If you don't have it installed, you can install it via pip:

Next, we'll write the Python code for the ETL application. We'll use the OpenSky API to get real-time flight data. Sign up for an API key from the OpenSky website (it's free). Replace {{your_username}} and {{your_password}} in the code with the OpenSky login credentials.

```python
import requests
import json
import sqlite3
from sqlalchemy import create_engine, Column, Integer, String, Float
from sqlalchemy.ext.declarative import declarative_base
from sqlalchemy.orm import sessionmaker

# OpenSky API credentials
USERNAME = "{{your_username}}"
PASSWORD = "{{your_password}}"

# SQLite database name
DB_NAME = "flights_db.sqlite"

# SQLAlchemy setup
Base = declarative_base()
engine = create_engine(f'sqlite:///{DB_NAME}')
Session = sessionmaker(bind=engine)
session = Session()

# Flight model
class Flight(Base):
    __tablename__ = "flights"

    id = Column(Integer, primary_key=True)
    icao24 = Column(String)
    callsign = Column(String)
    lat = Column(Float)
    lon = Column(Float)
    alt = Column(Float)

# Create table schema
Base.metadata.create_all(engine)

# Get flight data
def get_flights_near_london():
    url = f"https://opensky-network.org/api/states/all?lamin=51.29&lomin=-0.65&lamax=51.75&lomax=0.55"
    response = requests.get(url, auth=(USERNAME, PASSWORD))
    data = json.loads(response.text)
    
    # Limit the flights to 100 if more flights were retrieved.
    if len(data["states"]) > 100:
        data["states"] = data["states"][:100]
    
    return data["states"]

# ETL process
def etl_process(flights):
    # Truncate flights table
    session.query(Flight).delete()
    session.commit()

    # Insert flights data
    for flight_data in flights:
        flight = Flight(
            icao24=flight_data[0],
            callsign=flight_data[1],
            lat=flight_data[6],
            lon=flight_data[5],
            alt=flight_data[7]
        )

        session.add(flight)
    session.commit()

# Run the ETL
if __name__ == "__main__":
    flights_data = get_flights_near_london()
    etl_process(flights_data)
```

Save this Python code in a file called `london_flights_etl.py`.

To run the code, open a terminal (or command prompt) and navigate to the directory containing the `london_flights_etl.py` file. Then, simply run the following command:

```bash
python london_flights_etl.py
```

This will create an SQLite database file named `flights_db.sqlite` containing the flight information. The ETL process in the code first deletes any existing flight data from the database and then inserts the new flight data.

```python
import os
import requests
import json
from sqlalchemy import create_engine, Column, Integer, String, Float
from sqlalchemy.ext.declarative import declarative_base
from sqlalchemy.orm import sessionmaker

# Set environment variable to silence SQLAlchemy warning
os.environ["SQLALCHEMY_SILENCE_UBER_WARNING"] = "1"

# SQLite database name
DB_NAME = "flights_db.sqlite"

# SQLAlchemy setup
Base = declarative_base()
engine = create_engine(f'sqlite:///{DB_NAME}')
Session = sessionmaker(bind=engine)
session = Session()

# Flight model
class Flight(Base):
    __tablename__ = "flights"

    id = Column(Integer, primary_key=True)
    icao24 = Column(String)
    callsign = Column(String)
    lat = Column(Float)
    lon = Column(Float)
    alt = Column(Float)

# Create table schema
Base.metadata.create_all(engine)

# Get flight data
def get_flights_near_london():
    url = "https://adsbexchange-com1.p.rapidapi.com/json/lat/51.50/lng/-0.11/dist/30/"
    headers = {
        "X-RapidAPI-Key": "f5d2e2aaedomshb6d727ed6a8d6ap1e86e9jsn13261willremainconfidential",
        "X-RapidAPI-Host": "adsbexchange-com1.p.rapidapi.com"
    }
    response = requests.get(url, headers=headers)

    if response.status_code != 200:
        print(f"Error fetching flight data: {response.status_code}")
        return []

    data = json.loads(response.text)

    # Limit the flights to 100 if more flights were retrieved.
    if len(data["ac"]) > 100:
        data["ac"] = data["ac"][:100]

    return data["ac"]

# ETL process
def etl_process(flights):
    # Truncate flights table
    session.query(Flight).delete()
    session.commit()

    # Insert flights data
    for flight_data in flights:
        flight = Flight(
            icao24=flight_data["icao"],
            callsign=flight_data["call"],
            lat=flight_data["lat"],
            lon=flight_data["lon"],
            alt=flight_data["alt"]
        )

        session.add(flight)
    session.commit()

# Run the ETL
if __name__ == "__main__":
    flights_data = get_flights_near_london()
    etl_process(flights_data)
```

2023-03-22 15:56:25 [ASSISTANT] To create a Python library called `rest-to-sql` which takes API endpoints as input and automatically converts them to SQL tables, we can follow these steps. In this example, we'll use the TFL (Transport for London) API.

First, let's install the necessary dependencies:

```bash
pip install requests pandas sqlalchemy
```

Now, let's create a file called `rest_to_sql.py`:

```python
import requests
import pandas as pd
import sqlalchemy as db

def extract_data_from_api(url, params):
    response = requests.get(url, params=params)
    return response.json()

def json_to_dataframe(data):
    return pd.json_normalize(data)

def connect_to_database(db_url):
    engine = db.create_engine(db_url)
    connection = engine.connect()
    return engine, connection

def push_data_to_table(df, table_name, engine):
    df.to_sql(table_name, engine, if_exists='replace', index=False)

def main(tfl_api_key, tfl_app_id, db_url):
    # TFL API URLs and parameters
    urls = {
        'tfl_stats': 'https://api.tfl.gov.uk/bikepoint',
    }

    params = {
        'tfl_stats': {'app_key': tfl_api_key, 'app_id': tfl_app_id},
    }

    # Obtain data from TFL API
    tfl_data = extract_data_from_api(urls['tfl_stats'], params['tfl_stats'])

    # Process the JSON data into a DataFrame
    tfl_df = json_to_dataframe(tfl_data)

    # Connect to the database
    engine, connection = connect_to_database(db_url)

    # Push the data to database table
    push_data_to_table(tfl_df, "tfl_bikepoints", engine)

    # Close connection
    connection.close()

if __name__ == '__main__':
    # Add your TFL API key, TFL App ID, and database URL in the following format:
    # postgresql://username:password@localhost/db_name
    tfl_api_key = "your_tfl_api_key"
    tfl_app_id = "your_tfl_app_id"
    db_url = "your_database_url"
    main(tfl_api_key, tfl_app_id, db_url)
```

To run the code, replace `your_tfl_api_key`, `your_tfl_app_id` and `your_database_url` with your TFL API credentials and database URL respectively.

The code takes the TFL Bikepoints API as an example and converts the data into an SQL table called `tfl_bikepoints`. You can extend this by adding more API endpoints from different sources and updating the `urls` and `params` dictionaries.

To execute the script, run:

```bash
python rest_to_sql.py
```

This script will create an SQL table called `tfl_bikepoints` in the specified database, which contains the information retrieved from the TFL Bikepoints API.

2023-03-22 15:59:10 [ASSISTANT] I apologize for the inconvenience. It seems there is an issue with the API request or response. I've added error handling to the code to address this issue. Please try the following updated code:

```python
import requests
import pandas as pd
import sqlalchemy as db

def extract_data_from_api(url, params):
    try:
        response = requests.get(url, params=params)
        response.raise_for_status()
        return response.json()
    except requests.exceptions.HTTPError as err:
        raise SystemExit(err)

def json_to_dataframe(data):
    return pd.json_normalize(data)

def connect_to_database(db_url):
    engine = db.create_engine(db_url)
    connection = engine.connect()
    return engine, connection

def push_data_to_table(df, table_name, engine):
    df.to_sql(table_name, engine, if_exists='replace', index=False)

def main(tfl_api_key, tfl_app_id, db_url):
    # TFL API URLs and parameters
    urls = {
        'tfl_stats': 'https://api.tfl.gov.uk/bikepoint',
    }

    params = {
        'tfl_stats': {'app_key': tfl_api_key, 'app_id': tfl_app_id},
    }

    # Obtain data from TFL API
    tfl_data = extract_data_from_api(urls['tfl_stats'], params['tfl_stats'])

    # Process the JSON data into a DataFrame
    tfl_df = json_to_dataframe(tfl_data)

    # Connect to the database
    engine, connection = connect_to_database(db_url)

    # Push the data to database table
    push_data_to_table(tfl_df, "tfl_bikepoints", engine)

    # Close connection
    connection.close()

if __name__ == '__main__':
    # Add your TFL API key, TFL App ID, and database URL in the following format:
    # postgresql://username:password@localhost/db_name
    tfl_api_key = "your_tfl_api_key"
    tfl_app_id = "your_tfl_app_id"
    db_url = "your_database_url"
    main(tfl_api_key, tfl_app_id, db_url)
```

2023-03-22 16:01:58 [ASSISTANT] To create a Python library called `rest-to-sql`, we can use the `requests` library to interact with APIs and `sqlite3` to store the data in SQL tables. We're using public APIs that provide data about London, e.g., Transport for London (TfL) API for bike points and the UK Police API for street-level crime data.

Install the required dependencies using the following command:

```bash
pip install requests
```

Create a new Python file, `rest_to_sql.py`, and paste the following code:

```python
import requests
import sqlite3

def create_bike_points_table(conn):
    conn.execute('CREATE TABLE IF NOT EXISTS bike_points (id INTEGER PRIMARY KEY, name TEXT, lat REAL, lon REAL)')

def create_crime_table(conn):
    conn.execute('CREATE TABLE IF NOT EXISTS crimes (id INTEGER PRIMARY KEY AUTOINCREMENT, category TEXT, location_lon REAL, location_lat REAL, street_name TEXT, outcome_status TEXT, date TEXT)')

def insert_bike_point(conn, point):
    conn.execute('INSERT INTO bike_points (id, name, lat, lon) VALUES (?, ?, ?, ?)', (point['id'], point['commonName'], point['lat'], point['lon']))
    conn.commit()

def insert_crime(conn, crime):
    conn.execute('INSERT INTO crimes (category, location_lon, location_lat, street_name, outcome_status, date) VALUES (?, ?, ?, ?, ?, ?)', (crime['category'], crime['location']['longitude'], crime['location']['latitude'], crime['location']['street']['name'], crime['outcome_status']['category'], crime['month']))
    conn.commit()

def load_bike_points(conn):
    response = requests.get('https://api.tfl.gov.uk/BikePoint')
    
    if response.status_code != 200:
        print('Failed to load TfL bike points.')
        return
    
    data = response.json()
    
    for item in data:
        point = {
            'id': item['id'].split('_')[-1],
            'commonName': item['commonName'],
            'lat': item['lat'],
            'lon': item['lon']
        }
        insert_bike_point(conn, point)

def load_crime_data(conn):
    payload = {
        'lat': '51.5074',
        'lng': '-0.1277',
        'date': '2020-01'
    }
    response = requests.get('https://data.police.uk/api/crimes-street/all-crime', params=payload)
    
    if response.status_code != 200:
        print('Failed to load UK Police street-level crime data.')
        return
    
    crime_data = response.json()

    for crime in crime_data:
        if 'category' in crime and 'location' in crime and 'latitude' in crime['location'] and 'longitude' in crime['location']:
            insert_crime(conn, crime)

def main():    
    with sqlite3.connect('london_data.db') as conn:
        print('Creating tables...')
        create_bike_points_table(conn)
        create_crime_table(conn)
        
        print('Loading TfL bike points...')
        load_bike_points(conn)
        
        print('Loading UK Police street-level crime data...')
        load_crime_data(conn)

        print('Finished loading data.')

main()
```

Run the `rest_to_sql.py` script in the terminal:

```bash
python rest_to_sql.py
```

This code creates an SQLite database file called `london_data.db` containing two tables, `bike_points` and `crimes`. The script fetches the bike points from the TfL API and the street-level crime data from the UK Police API, and inserts the data into their respective tables.

Note that this data only sample data for the given longitude and latitude points for crime data, and you might want to adapt these points to your needs. Also, you may want to extend the library to support more APIs or additional functionality such as updates, and query filters. Remember this code is just an example and not covering several aspects of production use cases like error handling, etc.

2023-03-22 16:06:35 [ASSISTANT] Creating the entire library would require a lot of code, but I can provide you with a basic outline of how you could create a simple Python library to fetch data from public APIs and convert them to SQL tables using SQLAlchemy. I'll use London Air Quality API as an example.

First, install the required dependencies using pip:

```bash
pip install requests sqlalchemy flask pandas
```

Next, create the Python code to fetch data from the API, convert it to an SQL table, and refresh the table every minute:

```python
import requests
import time
import pandas as pd
from sqlalchemy import create_engine, Column, Integer, String, DateTime, Float
from sqlalchemy.ext.declarative import declarative_base
from sqlalchemy.orm import sessionmaker
from flask import Flask, render_template_string

Base = declarative_base()

# Define the model for the air quality table
class AirQualityLdn(Base):
    __tablename__ = 'air_quality_ldn'
    id = Column(Integer, primary_key=True)
    date = Column(DateTime, nullable=False)
    no2 = Column(Float, nullable=False)
    particulate_matter = Column(Float, nullable=False)

# Create the database and table
engine = create_engine('sqlite:///london_air_quality.db')
Base.metadata.create_all(engine)
Session = sessionmaker(bind=engine)

# Fetch data and update the table
def update_air_quality_table():
    url = "https://api.erg.ic.ac.uk/AirQuality/Data/Nowcast/Index/1/Hourly/"
    response = requests.get(url)
    data = response.json()
    date = pd.to_datetime(data["AirQualityIndexObs"][0]["@MeasurementDateGMT"])
    no2 = float(data["AirQualityIndexObs"][0]["Species"][0]["@AirQualityIndex"])
    particulate_matter = float(data["AirQualityIndexObs"][0]["Species"][1]["@AirQualityIndex"])

    new_entry = AirQualityLdn(date=date, no2=no2, particulate_matter=particulate_matter)

    session = Session()
    session.add(new_entry)
    session.commit()
    session.close()

# Schedule table updates
def update_table_every_minute():
    while True:
        update_air_quality_table()
        time.sleep(60)

# Start the Flask app to display data on a web page
app = Flask(__name__)

@app.route('/')
def index():
    session = Session()
    data = session.query(AirQualityLdn).all()
    session.close()
    return render_template_string('''
        <table>
            <tr>
                <th>Date</th>
                <th>NO2</th>
                <th>Particulate Matter</th>
            </tr>
            {% for entry in data %}
            <tr>
                <td>{{ entry.date }}</td>
                <td>{{ entry.no2 }}</td>
                <td>{{ entry.particulate_matter }}</td>
            </tr>
            {% endfor %}
        </table>
    ''', data=data)

if __name__ == '__main__':
    # Start the table update thread in the background and the Flask app
    import threading
    update_table_thread = threading.Thread(target=update_table_every_minute, daemon=True)
    update_table_thread.start()
    
    app.run(debug=True)
```

Save this code in a Python file (e.g., `rest_to_sql_london_air_quality.py`) and run it using `python rest_to_sql_london_air_quality.py`. Access the web page at `http://127.0.0.1:5000/` to see the air quality data displayed in a table.

Please note that this is a sample implementation and can be improved depending on your specific requirements. For creating the library, you can modularize the code and create separate files for models, API calls, and the web application.

2023-03-22 16:10:57 [ASSISTANT] For this example, we will use a public API provided by OpenSky Network to fetch live data of flights near London. We'll use requests, pandas, and SQLite3 to process and store the extracted data. Make sure you have the dependencies installed. You can install them by running the following command:

```bash
pip install requests pandas
```

Here's the Python code for the ETL application:

```python
import requests
import pandas as pd
import sqlite3
from sqlite3 import Error

# Extract
OPENSKY_API = 'https://opensky-network.org/api/states/all?'

def get_flights_near_london():
    # Approximately define the bounding box around London
    params = {
        'lamin': 50.92,
        'lomin': -0.51,
        'lamax': 51.58,
        'lomax': 0.62,
    }
    response = requests.get(OPENSKY_API, params=params)
    data = response.json()

    flights = data['states']
    columns = [
        'icao24', 'callsign', 'origin_country',
        'longitude', 'latitude',
        'altitude', 'on_ground', 'velocity',
        'heading', 'vertical_rate', 'sensors',
        'baro_altitude', 'squawk', 'spi',
        'position_source'
    ]
    return pd.DataFrame(flights, columns=columns)

# Transform
def clean_flights_data(flights_df):
    # Clean the callsigns and remove any unnecessary spaces
    flights_df['callsign'] = flights_df['callsign'].str.strip()

    # Drop the unnecessary columns
    flights_df.drop(['sensors', 'spi', 'position_source'], axis=1, inplace=True)

    return flights_df

# Load
def create_connection(db_file):
    conn = None
    try:
        conn = sqlite3.connect(db_file)
    except Error as e:
        print(e)
    return conn

def save_flights_to_db(flights, db_file):
    conn = create_connection(db_file)

    if conn is not None:
        with conn:
            flights.to_sql('flights_near_london', conn, if_exists='replace', index=False)
    else:
        print("Cannot create database connection.")

def main():
    flights_df = get_flights_near_london()
    cleaned_flights = clean_flights_data(flights_df)
    save_flights_to_db(cleaned_flights, 'flights_db.sqlite')

if __name__ == '__main__':
    main()
```

To run the application, save the Python code as `flights_near_london_etl.py` and execute it using the command:

```bash
python flights_near_london_etl.py
```

This ETL application will extract data from the OpenSky API, remove unnecessary columns, clean up the callsign data, and save the resulting DataFrame to an SQLite database called `flights_db.sqlite`. The table in the SQLite database is named `flights_near_london`.

2023-03-22 16:18:04 [ASSISTANT] Let's set up a simple ETL application to retrieve flight information near London and store it in a SQLite database. We will use the `requests` library to fetch flight data from the OpenSky Network API and `sqlite3` for database functionalities.

First, you may need to install the necessary dependencies:

```bash
pip install requests
```

Here is the full Python code for the ETL application:

```python
import requests
import sqlite3
from datetime import datetime

# Define API URL and parameters
API_URL = "https://opensky-network.org/api/states/all"
PARAMS = {
    "lamin": 50.988,
    "lomin": -0.722,
    "lamax": 51.6971,
    "lomax": 0.392,
}

# Create the SQLite database
conn = sqlite3.connect("flights_near_london.db")
cursor = conn.cursor()
cursor.execute("""CREATE TABLE IF NOT EXISTS flights (icao24 TEXT PRIMARY KEY, callsign TEXT,
                 origin_country TEXT, longitude REAL, latitude REAL, time timestamp)""")

def fetch_flights_near_london():
    response = requests.get(API_URL, params=PARAMS)
    data = response.json()

    return [
        {
            "icao24": flight[0],
            "callsign": flight[1].strip(),
            "origin_country": flight[2],
            "longitude": flight[5],
            "latitude": flight[6],
            "time": datetime.fromtimestamp(flight[3]),
        }
        for flight in data["states"]
        if flight[5] is not None and flight[6] is not None
    ]

def store_flights_in_db(flights):
    for flight in flights:
        cursor.execute("""INSERT OR REPLACE INTO flights (icao24, callsign, origin_country, 
                         longitude, latitude, time) VALUES (?, ?, ?, ?, ?, ?)""",
                         (flight["icao24"], flight["callsign"], flight["origin_country"],
                          flight["longitude"], flight["latitude"], flight["time"]))
    conn.commit()

if __name__ == "__main__":
    flights_near_london = fetch_flights_near_london()
    store_flights_in_db(flights_near_london)
    print("Flights near London have been saved to the SQLite database.")
    conn.close()
```

To run the code, simply execute the Python script:

```bash
python flights_near_london_etl.py
```

This script will create a SQLite database file named `flights_near_london.db` and store any flights near London retrieved from the OpenSky Network API. Please note that the OpenSky Network API does not require an API key. Let me know if you need any help or additional features!

2023-03-22 16:25:36 [ASSISTANT] To create a real-time data app in Python, we will use Flask, Socket.IO, and JavaScript. Flask is a web framework for creating web applications in Python, while Socket.IO is a library to handle real-time communication between the server and the client. The client-side will utilize JavaScript to update the data in real-time.

First, you need to install the following dependencies:

```bash
pip install flask flask-socketio
```
Then, create the application using Python and Flask. Save the following code in a file named `app.py`:

```python
from flask import Flask, render_template
from flask_socketio import SocketIO

app = Flask(__name__)
app.config['SECRET_KEY'] = 'your-secret-key'  # Replace with your secret key.
socketio = SocketIO(app)

@app.route('/')
def index():
    return render_template('index.html')

if __name__ == '__main__':
    socketio.run(app)
```

Next, create the corresponding `index.html` template file in a folder called `templates`. This HTML file will contain the JavaScript code for real-time communication using Socket.IO.

```html
<!DOCTYPE html>
<html lang="en">
<head>
    <meta charset="UTF-8">
    <meta name="viewport" content="width=device-width, initial-scale=1.0">
    <title>Real-Time Data App</title>
    <script src="https://cdnjs.cloudflare.com/ajax/libs/socket.io/4.1.2/socket.io.min.js"></script>
    <script>
        document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', () => {
            const socket = io.connect('http://' + document.domain + ':' + location.port);

            socket.on('connect', () => {
                console.log('Connected to server');
            });

            socket.on('update_data', (data) => {
                document.getElementById("data").innerHTML = JSON.stringify(data);
            })
        });
    </script>
</head>
<body>
    <h1>Real-Time Data</h1>
    <pre id="data"></pre>
</body>
</html>
```

Finally, update the Python code to generate and send real-time data to clients. Add this code to your `app.py`:

```python
import random
import time
from threading import Thread
from flask import jsonify

def update_data():
    while True:
        time.sleep(1)
        data = {
            'value': random.randint(0, 100),
            'timestamp': time.time()
        }
        socketio.emit('update_data', data)

@app.route('/')
def index():
    data_update_thread = Thread(target=update_data)
    data_update_thread.start()
    return render_template('index.html')
```

Now run the application:

```bash
python app.py
```

Open a web browser and navigate to `http://127.0.0.1:5000/`. The page will display the real-time data generated by the server.

Please make sure to separate the HTML and Python code into two separate files: `app.py` for Python code and `index.html` for the HTML template in the `templates` folder.

Here is the correct structure of the files:

1. `app.py` - This file contains the Python code for the Flask server:

```python
from flask import Flask, render_template
from flask_socketio import SocketIO
import random
import time
from threading import Thread

app = Flask(__name__)
app.config['SECRET_KEY'] = 'your-secret-key'  # Replace with your secret key.
socketio = SocketIO(app)

def update_data():
    while True:
        time.sleep(1)
        data = {
            'value': random.randint(0, 100),
            'timestamp': time.time()
        }
        socketio.emit('update_data', data)

@app.route('/')
def index():
    data_update_thread = Thread(target=update_data)
    data_update_thread.start()
    return render_template('index.html')

if __name__ == '__main__':
    socketio.run(app)
```

2. `templates/index.html` - This file contains the HTML template with embedded JavaScript code for real-time updates:

```html
<!DOCTYPE html>
<html lang="en">
<head>
    <meta charset="UTF-8">
    <meta name="viewport" content="width=device-width, initial-scale=1.0">
    <title>Real-Time Data App</title>
    <script src="https://cdnjs.cloudflare.com/ajax/libs/socket.io/4.1.2/socket.io.min.js"></script>
    <script>
        document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', () => {
            const socket = io.connect('http://' + document.domain + ':' + location.port);

            socket.on('connect', () => {
                console.log('Connected to server');
            });

            socket.on('update_data', (data) => {
                document.getElementById("data").innerHTML = JSON.stringify(data);
            })
        });
    </script>
</head>
<body>
    <h1>Real-Time Data</h1>
    <pre id="data"></pre>
</body>
</html>
```

Make sure to remove the HTML code from the `app.py` file and save it in a separate file named `index.html` inside the `templates` folder. Then run the application again with `python app.py`, and it should work as expected.

2023-03-22 16:33:21 [ASSISTANT] To get the last Bored Ape Yacht Club (BAYC) sold, you can use the OpenSea API to fetch the most recent sales data. Here's a script that fetches NFT sales data for BAYC and prints the most recent sale:

```bash
pip install requests
```

```python
import requests

def get_last_bayc_sale():
    url = "https://api.opensea.io/api/v1/events"
    query = {
        "event_type": "successful",
        "only_opensea": "false",
        "offset": "0",
        "limit": "1",
        "collection_slug": "boredapeyachtclub",
    }
    headers = {
        "Accept": "application/json",
    }
    
    response = requests.get(url, headers=headers, params=query)
    data = response.json()
    
    if data and data.get("asset_events"):
        last_sale = data["asset_events"][0]
        sale_price = int(last_sale["total_price"]) / 1e18
        sale_data = {
            "name": last_sale["asset"]["name"],
            "token_id": last_sale["asset"]["token_id"],
            "price": sale_price,
            "timestamp": last_sale["transaction"]["timestamp"],
        }
        return sale_data
    else:
        print("No BAYC sales found.")
        return None

if __name__ == "__main__":
    last_bayc_sale = get_last_bayc_sale()
    if last_bayc_sale:
        print(f"Last BAYC Sold: {last_bayc_sale['name']}")
        print(f"Token ID: {last_bayc_sale['token_id']}")
        print(f"Price: {last_bayc_sale['price']}ETH")
        print(f"Timestamp: {last_bayc_sale['timestamp']}")
```

Make sure you have `requests` library installed using `pip install requests`, then simply execute the Python script. The script will print the recent BAYC sale including the name, token_id, price, and timestamp of the sale.

2023-03-22 16:38:25 [ASSISTANT] To get the last Bored Ape Yacht Club (BAYC) NFT sold, we'll use the OpenSea API. No API key is required for public data.

Install the required dependency:

```bash
pip install requests
```

Python code:

```python
import requests
import json

def get_last_bayc_sold():
    url = "https://api.opensea.io/api/v1/events"
    query = {
        "event_type": "successful",
        "only_opensea": "false",
        "offset": "0",
        "limit": "1",
        "collection_slug": "boredapeyachtclub",
    }
    response = requests.get(url, params=query)
    data = response.json()

    if len(data["asset_events"]) > 0:
        last_sold = data["asset_events"][0]
        return {
            "token_id": last_sold["asset"]["token_id"],
            "name": last_sold["asset"]["name"],
            "image_url": last_sold["asset"]["image_url"],
            "price_in_eth": float(last_sold["total_price"]) / (10 ** 18),
            "transaction_timestamp": last_sold["transaction"]["timestamp"],
        }
    else:
        return None

if __name__ == "__main__":
    last_bayc = get_last_bayc_sold()
    if last_bayc:
        print(f"Last BAYC NFT sold:\n"
              f"Token ID: {last_bayc['token_id']}\n"
              f"Name: {last_bayc['name']}\n"
              f"Image URL: {last_bayc['image_url']}\n"
              f"Price in ETH: {last_bayc['price_in_eth']}\n"
              f"Transaction Timestamp: {last_bayc['transaction_timestamp']}")
    else:
        print("No recent BAYC NFT sales found.")
```

Instructions to run the code:

1. Install the required dependency using `pip install requests`.
2. Copy the Python code to a file named `last_bayc_sold.py`.
3. Run the Python script with the command `python last_bayc_sold.py`.
4. The last BAYC NFT sold information will be displayed on the console.

```python
import requests
import json

def get_last_bayc_sold():
    url = "https://api.opensea.io/api/v1/events"
    query = {
        "event_type": "successful",
        "only_opensea": "false",
        "offset": "0",
        "limit": "1",
        "collection_slug": "boredapeyachtclub",
    }
    response = requests.get(url, params=query)
    
    try:
        data = response.json()
    except json.JSONDecodeError:
        print("Error: Unable to parse API response as JSON.")
        return None

    if len(data["asset_events"]) > 0:
        last_sold = data["asset_events"][0]
        return {
            "token_id": last_sold["asset"]["token_id"],
            "name": last_sold["asset"]["name"],
            "image_url": last_sold["asset"]["image_url"],
            "price_in_eth": float(last_sold["total_price"]) / (10 ** 18),
            "transaction_timestamp": last_sold["transaction"]["timestamp"],
        }
    else:
        return None

if __name__ == "__main__":
    last_bayc = get_last_bayc_sold()
    if last_bayc:

              f"Token ID: {last_bayc['token_id']}\n"
```

```python
import requests
import json

def get_last_bayc_sold():
    url = "https://api.opensea.io/api/v1/events"
    query = {
        "event_type": "successful",
        "only_opensea": "false",
        "offset": "0",
        "limit": "1",
        "collection_slug": "boredapeyachtclub",
    }
    response = requests.get(url, params=query)

    if response.status_code != 200:
        print(f"Error: Unexpected API response. Status code: {response.status_code}")
        return None

    try:
        data = response.json()
    except json.JSONDecodeError:
        print("Error: Unable to parse API response as JSON.")
        return None

    if len(data["asset_events"]) > 0:
        last_sold = data["asset_events"][0]
        return {
            "token_id": last_sold["asset"]["token_id"],
            "name": last_sold["asset"]["name"],
            "image_url": last_sold["asset"]["image_url"],
            "price_in_eth": float(last_sold["total_price"]) / (10 ** 18),
            "transaction_timestamp": last_sold["transaction"]["timestamp"],
        }
    else:
        return None

if __name__ == "__main__":
    last_bayc = get_last_bayc_sold()
    if last_bayc:
        print(f"Last BAYC NFT sold:\n"
              f"Token ID: {last_bayc['token_id']}\n"
```

2023-03-22 16:44:05 [ASSISTANT] I apologize for the inconvenience. It seems like the OpenSea API is returning a 403 (Forbidden) status, which may be due to rate limiting or other restrictions. As a workaround, we can use the OpenSea's recently sold assets API and filter the results to display only Bored Ape Yacht Club (BAYC) sales.

Here's the updated code:

```python
import requests
import json

def get_last_bayc_sold():
    url = "https://api.opensea.io/api/v1/assets"
    query = {
        "order_by": "sale_date",
        "order_direction": "desc",
        "limit": "50",
    }
    response = requests.get(url, params=query)

    if response.status_code != 200:
        print(f"Error: Unexpected API response. Status code: {response.status_code}")
        return None

    try:
        data = response.json()
    except json.JSONDecodeError:
        print("Error: Unable to parse API response as JSON.")
        return None

    last_sold = None
    for asset in data["assets"]:
        if asset["collection"]["slug"] == "boredapeyachtclub":
            last_sold = asset
            break

    if last_sold is not None:
        return {
            "token_id": last_sold["token_id"],
            "name": last_sold["name"],
            "image_url": last_sold["image_url"],
            "price_in_eth": float(last_sold["last_sale"]["total_price"]) / (10 ** 18),
            "transaction_timestamp": last_sold["last_sale"]["event_timestamp"],
        }
    else:
        return None

if __name__ == "__main__":
    last_bayc = get_last_bayc_sold()
    if last_bayc:
        print(f"Last BAYC NFT sold:\n"
              f"Token ID: {last_bayc['token_id']}\n"
              f"Name: {last_bayc['name']}\n"
              f"Image URL: {last_bayc['image_url']}\n"
              f"Price in ETH: {last_bayc['price_in_eth']}\n"
              f"Transaction Timestamp: {last_bayc['transaction_timestamp']}")
    else:
        print("No recent BAYC NFT sales found.")
```

This code uses the assets API to retrieve the 50 most recently sold assets and filters the results to find the latest BAYC sale. To run this modified code, follow the instructions from the previous responses.

If there is still an issue, it may be due to temporary rate limiting or restrictions imposed by the OpenSea API. If that's the case, you might need to try running the script again after waiting for some time.

2023-03-22 16:48:46 [ASSISTANT] To calculate the average price per car and integrate it into the existing code, first, input the prices for each year manually. You can obtain historical data from various sources such as [AutoTrader](https://www.autotrader.co.uk/) and [Parkers](https://www.parkers.co.uk/). Note that this data is just an approximation and only serves demonstration purposes.
Next, use Python's statistics module to calculate the mean price. Finally, modify the code to display both the number of cars and average price trends in one plot.

First, install the required dependency if you haven't already:

```bash
pip install matplotlib
```

Now, here's the Python code to create the graph with both the number of cars and average price:

```python
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import statistics 

def main():
    years = [2016, 2017, 2018, 2019, 2020]
    number_of_cars = [31.7e6, 32.3e6, 32.5e6, 32.2e6, 31.9e6]
    avg_car_prices = [14500, 14625, 14700, 14830, 15020]

    avg_car_price = statistics.mean(avg_car_prices)

    fig, ax1 = plt.subplots()

    color = 'tab:blue'
    ax1.set_xlabel('Years')
    ax1.set_xticks(years)
    ax1.set_ylabel('Number of Cars (in millions)', color=color)
    ax1.plot(years, number_of_cars, marker='o', color=color)
    ax1.tick_params(axis='y', labelcolor=color)

    ax2 = ax1.twinx()

    color = 'tab:red'
    ax2.set_ylabel('Average Car Price (£)', color=color)
    ax2.plot(years, avg_car_prices, marker='o', color=color)
    ax2.tick_params(axis='y', labelcolor=color)

    plt.title(f'Number of Cars and Average Price in England (2016 - 2020)\nMean Price: £{avg_car_price}')
    fig.tight_layout()
    plt.grid()
    plt.show()

if __name__ == '__main__':
    main()
```

To run the code, save it as a .py file (e.g., `cars_and_prices.py`). Execute it using the Python interpreter:

```bash
python cars_and_prices.py
```

This will display a graph showing the number of cars and the average car price in England over the last 5 years with the mean price as part of the title.

2023-03-22 16:52:03 [ASSISTANT] Unfortunately, there aren't any specific public APIs available that provide both the number of cars and average car prices in England for the mentioned time frame. However, we can gather data from two separate APIs and combine them on our graph.

For car prices, we can use the NADA API.

```bash
pip install matplotlib requests
```

Then, use the following Python code to fetch the data and generate the graph:

```python
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import requests

def get_number_of_cars(year):
    url = f"https://api.transport.data.gov.uk/v1/car-counts/england/estimate?year={year}"
    response = requests.get(url)

    if response.status_code == 200:
        data = response.json()
        total_cars = data['data']['totalCars']
        return total_cars
    else:
        print(f"Error fetching number of cars for {year}: {response.status_code}")
        return None

def get_average_car_prices():
    # Replace this function with a call to an API that provides average car prices for England (if available).
    # Currently, there are no known public APIs providing this information.
    return [14500, 14625, 14700, 14830, 15020]

def main():
    years = [2016, 2017, 2018, 2019, 2020]
    number_of_cars = [get_number_of_cars(year) for year in years]
    avg_car_prices = get_average_car_prices()

    fig, ax1 = plt.subplots()

    color = 'tab:blue'
    ax1.set_xlabel('Years')
    ax1.set_xticks(years)
    ax1.set_ylabel('Number of Cars (in millions)', color=color)
    ax1.plot(years, number_of_cars, marker='o', color=color)
    ax1.tick_params(axis='y', labelcolor=color)

    ax2 = ax1.twinx()

    color = 'tab:red'
    ax2.set_ylabel('Average Car Price (£)', color=color)
    ax2.plot(years, avg_car_prices, marker='o', color=color)
    ax2.tick_params(axis='y', labelcolor=color)

    plt.title(f'Number of Cars and Average Price in England (2016 - 2020)')
    fig.tight_layout()
    plt.grid()
    plt.show()

if __name__ == '__main__':
    main()
```

Please note that the NADA API is specific to the US car market, and there are no known public APIs providing average car prices for England. The `get_average_car_prices()` function in the code is a placeholder that returns the previously entered average car prices. If you find an appropriate API for car prices in England, you can replace this function with a call to that API.
